<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Uranium</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the chemical element. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Uranium_(disambiguation)" title="Uranium (disambiguation)">Uranium (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">chemical element with atomic number 92</div><table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><caption><span style="white-space:nowrap">Uranium,  <sub><span style="font-size:smaller;">92</span></sub>U</span></caption><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HEUraniumC.jpg" title="Two hands in brown gloves holding a blotched gray disk with a number 2068 hand-written on it"><img alt="Two hands in brown gloves holding a blotched gray disk with a number 2068 hand-written on it" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="3200" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/HEUraniumC.jpg/220px-HEUraniumC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/HEUraniumC.jpg/330px-HEUraniumC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/HEUraniumC.jpg/440px-HEUraniumC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#ff99cc">General properties</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Pronunciation</th><td>
<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/ʊəˈr/: 'ur' in 'plurality'">ʊəˈr</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span></span>/</a></span></span> <wbr/>​<span class="nowrap">(<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling">ewr-<span style="font-size:90%">AY</span>-nee-əm</i></a>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Appearance</th><td>
silvery gray metallic; corrodes to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spalling" title="Spalling">spalling</a> black oxide coat in air</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Standard_atomic_weight" title="Standard atomic weight">Standard atomic weight</a><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r865336399">.mw-parser-output .nobold{font-weight:normal}</style><span class="nobold"> (<i>A</i><sub>r, standard</sub>)</span></th><td>

</td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr><tr><td class="noprint" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background:#ff99cc">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_uranium" title="Template:Infobox uranium"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_uranium" title="Template talk:Infobox uranium"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_uranium&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div> | <a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Uranium</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol <b>U</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 92. It is a silvery-grey <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinide</a> series of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table</a>. A uranium atom has 92 <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> and 92 <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a>, of which 6 are <a href="/wiki/Valence_electron" title="Valence electron">valence electrons</a>. Uranium is weakly <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a> because all <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_uranium" title="Isotopes of uranium">isotopes of uranium</a> are unstable, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half-lives" title="Half-lives">half-lives</a> varying between 159,200 years and 4.5 billion years. The most common isotopes in <a href="/wiki/Natural_uranium" title="Natural uranium">natural uranium</a> are <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">uranium-238</a> (which has 146 <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> and accounts for over 99%) and <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a> (which has 143 neutrons). Uranium has the highest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordially</a> occurring elements. Its <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> is about 70% higher than that of <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, and slightly lower than that of <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>. It occurs naturally in low concentrations of a few <a href="/wiki/Parts-per_notation#Parts-per_expressions" title="Parts-per notation">parts per million</a> in soil, rock and water, and is commercially <a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">extracted</a> from uranium-bearing <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Uraninite" title="Uraninite">uraninite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>In nature, uranium is found as uranium-238 (99.2739–99.2752%), uranium-235 (0.7198–0.7202%), and a very small amount of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-234" title="Uranium-234">uranium-234</a> (0.0050–0.0059%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Uranium decays slowly by emitting an <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a>. The half-life of uranium-238 is about 4.47 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a> years and that of uranium-235 is 704 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Million" title="Million">million</a> years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> making them useful in dating the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">age of the Earth</a>.
</p><p>Many contemporary uses of uranium exploit its unique <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclear</a> properties. Uranium-235 is the only naturally occurring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fissile" title="Fissile">fissile</a> <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a>, which makes it widely used in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>. However, because of the tiny amounts found in nature, uranium needs to undergo <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium_enrichment" title="Uranium enrichment">enrichment</a> so that enough uranium-235 is present. Uranium-238 is fissionable by fast neutrons, and is <a href="/wiki/Fertile_material" title="Fertile material">fertile</a>, meaning it can be <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmuted</a> to fissile <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactor</a>. Another fissile isotope, <a href="/wiki/Uranium-233" title="Uranium-233">uranium-233</a>, can be produced from natural <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a> and is also important in nuclear technology. Uranium-238 has a small probability for <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_fission" title="Spontaneous fission">spontaneous fission</a> or even induced fission with fast neutrons; uranium-235 and to a lesser degree uranium-233 have a much higher fission cross-section for slow neutrons. In sufficient concentration, these isotopes maintain a sustained <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a>. This generates the heat in nuclear power reactors, and produces the fissile material for nuclear weapons. <a href="/wiki/Depleted_uranium" title="Depleted uranium">Depleted uranium</a> (<sup>238</sup>U) is used in <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy_penetrator" title="Kinetic energy penetrator">kinetic energy penetrators</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_armour" title="Vehicle armour">armor plating</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks479_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks479-6">[6]</a></sup> Uranium is used as a colorant in <a href="/wiki/Uranium_glass" title="Uranium glass">uranium glass</a>, producing lemon yellow to green colors. Uranium glass fluoresces green in ultraviolet light. It was also used for tinting and shading in early <a href="/wiki/Photography" title="Photography">photography</a>.
</p><p>The 1789 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discovery_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Discovery of the chemical elements">discovery</a> of uranium in the mineral <a href="/wiki/Uraninite" title="Uraninite">pitchblende</a> is credited to <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth" title="Martin Heinrich Klaproth">Martin Heinrich Klaproth</a>, who named the new element after the recently discovered planet <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>. <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne-Melchior_P%C3%A9ligot" title="Eugène-Melchior Péligot">Eugène-Melchior Péligot</a> was the first person to isolate the metal and its radioactive properties were discovered in 1896 by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Becquerel" title="Henri Becquerel">Henri Becquerel</a>. Research by <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Otto Hahn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Lise Meitner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">Enrico Fermi</a> and others, such as <a href="/wiki/J._Robert_Oppenheimer" title="J. Robert Oppenheimer">J. Robert Oppenheimer</a> starting in 1934 led to its use as a fuel in the nuclear power industry and in <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Boy" title="Little Boy">Little Boy</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">first nuclear weapon used in war</a>. An ensuing <a href="/wiki/Arms_race" title="Arms race">arms race</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> between the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> produced tens of thousands of nuclear weapons that used uranium metal and uranium-derived <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a>. The security of those weapons and their fissile material following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collapse_of_the_Soviet_Union_(1985%E2%80%931991)#Dissolution_of_the_USSR" title="Collapse of the Soviet Union (1985–1991)">breakup of the Soviet Union</a> in 1991 is an ongoing concern for public health and safety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> See <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">Nuclear proliferation</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_fission.svg"><img alt="A diagram showing a chain transformation of uranium-235 to uranium-236 to barium-141 and krypton-92" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="309" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Nuclear_fission.svg/150px-Nuclear_fission.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Nuclear_fission.svg/225px-Nuclear_fission.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Nuclear_fission.svg/300px-Nuclear_fission.svg.png 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_fission.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A neutron-induced nuclear fission event involving uranium-235</div></div></div>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Refining_(metallurgy)" title="Refining (metallurgy)">refined</a>, uranium is a silvery white, weakly radioactive <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a>. It has a <a href="/wiki/Hardnesses_of_the_elements_(data_page)" title="Hardnesses of the elements (data page)">Mohs hardness</a> of 6, sufficient to scratch glass and approximately equal to that of <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhodium" title="Rhodium">rhodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Niobium" title="Niobium">niobium</a>. It is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malleability" title="Malleability">malleable</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a>, slightly <a href="/wiki/Paramagnetism" title="Paramagnetism">paramagnetic</a>, strongly <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electropositive</a> and a poor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciTechEncy_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-SciTechEncy-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup> Uranium metal has a very high <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of 19.1 g/cm<sup>3</sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> denser than <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> (11.3 g/cm<sup>3</sup>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> but slightly less dense than <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> (19.3 g/cm<sup>3</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Uranium metal reacts with almost all non-metal elements (with the exception of the <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gases</a>) and their <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">compounds</a>, with reactivity increasing with temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbiaEncy_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColumbiaEncy-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">Hydrochloric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acids</a> dissolve uranium, but non-oxidizing acids other than hydrochloric acid attack the element very slowly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciTechEncy_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-SciTechEncy-8">[8]</a></sup> When finely divided, it can react with cold water; in air, uranium metal becomes coated with a dark layer of <a href="/wiki/Uranium_oxide" title="Uranium oxide">uranium oxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup> Uranium in ores is extracted chemically and converted into <a href="/wiki/Uranium_dioxide" title="Uranium dioxide">uranium dioxide</a> or other chemical forms usable in industry.
</p><p>Uranium-235 was the first isotope that was found to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fissile" title="Fissile">fissile</a>. Other naturally occurring isotopes are fissionable, but not fissile. On bombardment with slow neutrons, its uranium-235 <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> will most of the time divide into two smaller <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclei</a>, releasing nuclear <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> and more neutrons. If too many of these neutrons are absorbed by other uranium-235 nuclei, a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a> occurs that results in a burst of heat or (in special circumstances) an explosion. In a nuclear reactor, such a chain reaction is slowed and controlled by a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_poison" title="Neutron poison">neutron poison</a>, absorbing some of the free neutrons. Such neutron absorbent materials are often part of reactor <a href="/wiki/Control_rod" title="Control rod">control rods</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor_physics" title="Nuclear reactor physics">nuclear reactor physics</a> for a description of this process of reactor control).
</p><p>As little as 15 lb (7 kg) of uranium-235 can be used to make an atomic bomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyIntel_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncyIntel-15">[15]</a></sup> The first nuclear bomb used in war, <i><a href="/wiki/Little_Boy" title="Little Boy">Little Boy</a></i>, relied on uranium fission, but the very first nuclear explosive (the <i>Gadget</i> used at <a href="/wiki/Trinity_(nuclear_test)" title="Trinity (nuclear test)">Trinity</a>) and the bomb that destroyed Nagasaki (<i><a href="/wiki/Fat_Man" title="Fat Man">Fat Man</a></i>) were both plutonium bombs.
</p><p>Uranium metal has three <a href="/wiki/Allotropy" title="Allotropy">allotropic</a> forms:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>α (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthorhombic" title="Orthorhombic">orthorhombic</a>) stable up to 668 °C. Orthorhombic, <a href="/wiki/Space_group" title="Space group">space group</a> No. 63, <i>Cmcm</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lattice_parameter" title="Lattice parameter">lattice parameters</a> <i>a</i> = 285.4 pm, <i>b</i> = 587 pm, <i>c</i> = 495.5 pm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grenthe_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grenthe-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li>β (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tetragonal" title="Tetragonal">tetragonal</a>) stable from 668 °C to 775 °C. Tetragonal, space group <i>P</i>4<sub>2</sub>/<i>mnm</i>, <i>P</i>4<sub>2</sub><i>nm</i>, or <i>P</i>4<i>n</i>2, lattice parameters <i>a</i> = 565.6 pm, <i>b</i> = <i>c</i> = 1075.9 pm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grenthe_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grenthe-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
<li>γ (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body-centered_cubic" title="Body-centered cubic">body-centered cubic</a>) from 775 °C to melting point—this is the most malleable and ductile state. Body-centered cubic, lattice parameter <i>a</i> = 352.4 pm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grenthe_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Grenthe-17">[17]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:30mm_DU_slug.jpg"><img alt="Shiny metallic cylinder with a sharpened tip. The overall length is 9 cm and diameter about 2 cm." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="431" data-file-width="540" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/30mm_DU_slug.jpg/220px-30mm_DU_slug.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/30mm_DU_slug.jpg/330px-30mm_DU_slug.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/30mm_DU_slug.jpg/440px-30mm_DU_slug.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:30mm_DU_slug.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Various militaries use <a href="/wiki/Depleted_uranium" title="Depleted uranium">depleted uranium</a> as high-density penetrators.</div></div></div>
<p>The major application of uranium in the military sector is in high-density penetrators. This ammunition consists of <a href="/wiki/Depleted_uranium" title="Depleted uranium">depleted uranium</a> (DU) alloyed with 1–2% other elements, such as <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> At high impact speed, the density, hardness, and <a href="/wiki/Pyrophoricity" title="Pyrophoricity">pyrophoricity</a> of the projectile enable the destruction of heavily armored targets. Tank armor and other removable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vehicle_armor" title="Vehicle armor">vehicle armor</a> can also be hardened with depleted uranium plates. The use of depleted uranium became politically and environmentally contentious after the use of such munitions by the US, UK and other countries during wars in the Persian Gulf and the Balkans raised questions concerning uranium compounds left in the soil (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_War_Syndrome" title="Gulf War Syndrome">Gulf War Syndrome</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyIntel_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-EncyIntel-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Depleted uranium is also used as a shielding material in some containers used to store and transport radioactive materials. While the metal itself is radioactive, its high density makes it more effective than <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> in halting radiation from strong sources such as <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciTechEncy_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-SciTechEncy-8">[8]</a></sup> Other uses of depleted uranium include counterweights for aircraft control surfaces, as ballast for missile <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_reentry" title="Atmospheric reentry">re-entry vehicles</a> and as a shielding material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup> Due to its high density, this material is found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inertial_guidance_system" title="Inertial guidance system">inertial guidance systems</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Gyroscope" title="Gyroscope">gyroscopic</a> <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compasses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup> Depleted uranium is preferred over similarly dense metals due to its ability to be easily machined and cast as well as its relatively low cost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks480_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks480-19">[19]</a></sup> The main risk of exposure to depleted uranium is chemical poisoning by <a href="/wiki/Uranium_oxide" title="Uranium oxide">uranium oxide</a> rather than radioactivity (uranium being only a weak <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha emitter</a>).
</p><p>During the later stages of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the entire <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, and to a lesser extent afterwards, uranium-235 has been used as the fissile explosive material to produce nuclear weapons. Initially, two major types of fission bombs were built: a relatively simple device that uses uranium-235 and a more complicated mechanism that uses <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a> derived from uranium-238. Later, a much more complicated and far more powerful type of fission/fusion bomb (<a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_weapon" title="Thermonuclear weapon">thermonuclear weapon</a>) was built, that uses a plutonium-based device to cause a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> to undergo <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>. Such bombs are jacketed in a non-fissile (unenriched) uranium case, and they derive more than half their power from the fission of this material by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fast_neutron" title="Fast neutron">fast neutrons</a> from the nuclear fusion process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civilian">Civilian</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Power_Plant_2.jpg"><img alt="Photograph featuring sunflowers in front and a plant on the back. The plant has a wide smoking chimney with diameter comparable to its height." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="218" data-file-width="288" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Nuclear_Power_Plant_2.jpg/220px-Nuclear_Power_Plant_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/db/Nuclear_Power_Plant_2.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_Power_Plant_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The most visible civilian use of uranium is as the thermal power source used in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plants</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The main use of uranium in the civilian sector is to fuel <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_plant" title="Nuclear power plant">nuclear power plants</a>. One kilogram of uranium-235 can theoretically produce about <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(energy)" title="Orders of magnitude (energy)">20 terajoules</a> of energy (2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">13</span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joules</a>), assuming complete fission; as much <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> as 1500 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks479_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks479-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commercial <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> plants use fuel that is typically enriched to around 3% uranium-235.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks479_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks479-6">[6]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/CANDU_reactor" title="CANDU reactor">CANDU</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnox" title="Magnox">Magnox</a> designs are the only commercial reactors capable of using unenriched uranium fuel. Fuel used for <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> reactors is typically highly enriched in <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a> (the exact values are <a href="/wiki/Classified_information" title="Classified information">classified</a>). In a <a href="/wiki/Breeder_reactor" title="Breeder reactor">breeder reactor</a>, uranium-238 can also be converted into <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> through the following reaction:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">238</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">92</sub></span>U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span> + n <span class="texhtml texhtml-big" style="font-size:165%;">→</span> <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium-239" title="Uranium-239"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">239</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">92</sub></span>U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span> + <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">γ</span> <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;"><i>β</i><sup>−</sup></span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;"><span class="texhtml texhtml-big" style="font-size:165%;">→</span></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;"> </span></span></span> <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neptunium-239" title="Neptunium-239"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">239</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">93</sub></span>Np<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span> <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; vertical-align:middle; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; font-size:70%; padding:0 0.1em;"><i>β</i><sup>−</sup></span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;"><span class="texhtml texhtml-big" style="font-size:165%;">→</span></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em; padding:0 0.1em;"> </span></span></span> <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">239</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">94</sub></span>Pu<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:U_glass_with_black_light.jpg"><img alt="A glass place on a glass stand. The plate is glowing green while the stand is colorless." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/U_glass_with_black_light.jpg/220px-U_glass_with_black_light.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/U_glass_with_black_light.jpg/330px-U_glass_with_black_light.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/U_glass_with_black_light.jpg/440px-U_glass_with_black_light.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:U_glass_with_black_light.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uranium glass glowing under <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UV light</a></div></div></div>
<p>Before (and, occasionally, after) the discovery of radioactivity, uranium was primarily used in small amounts for yellow glass and pottery glazes, such as <a href="/wiki/Uranium_glass" title="Uranium glass">uranium glass</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Fiesta_(dinnerware)" title="Fiesta (dinnerware)">Fiestaware</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The discovery and isolation of <a href="/wiki/Radium" title="Radium">radium</a> in uranium ore (pitchblende) by <a href="/wiki/Marie_Curie" title="Marie Curie">Marie Curie</a> sparked the development of uranium mining to extract the radium, which was used to make glow-in-the-dark paints for clock and aircraft dials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> This left a prodigious quantity of uranium as a waste product, since it takes three tonnes of uranium to extract one <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">gram</a> of radium. This waste product was diverted to the glazing industry, making uranium glazes very inexpensive and abundant. Besides the pottery glazes, <a href="/wiki/Uranium_tile" title="Uranium tile">uranium tile</a> glazes accounted for the bulk of the use, including common bathroom and kitchen tiles which can be produced in green, yellow, <a href="/wiki/Mauve" title="Mauve">mauve</a>, black, blue, red and other colors.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vacuum_capacitor_with_uranium_glass.jpg"><img alt="A glass cylinder capped on both ends with metal electrodes. Inside the glass bulb there is a metal cylinder connected to the electrodes." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1360" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Vacuum_capacitor_with_uranium_glass.jpg/220px-Vacuum_capacitor_with_uranium_glass.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Vacuum_capacitor_with_uranium_glass.jpg/330px-Vacuum_capacitor_with_uranium_glass.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Vacuum_capacitor_with_uranium_glass.jpg/440px-Vacuum_capacitor_with_uranium_glass.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vacuum_capacitor_with_uranium_glass.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uranium glass used as lead-in seals in a vacuum <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">capacitor</a></div></div></div>
<p>Uranium was also used in <a href="/wiki/Photography" title="Photography">photographic</a> chemicals (especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium_nitrate" title="Uranium nitrate">uranium nitrate</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Toner" title="Toner">toner</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup> in lamp filaments for <a href="/wiki/Stage_lighting" title="Stage lighting">stage lighting</a> bulbs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> to improve the appearance of <a href="/wiki/Dentures" title="Dentures">dentures</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and in the leather and wood industries for stains and dyes. Uranium salts are <a href="/wiki/Mordant" title="Mordant">mordants</a> of silk or wool. Uranyl acetate and uranyl formate are used as electron-dense "stains" in <a href="/wiki/Transmission_electron_microscopy" title="Transmission electron microscopy">transmission electron microscopy</a>, to increase the contrast of biological specimens in ultrathin sections and in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_staining" title="Negative staining">negative staining</a> of <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>, isolated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cell_organelle" title="Cell organelle">cell organelles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macromolecule" title="Macromolecule">macromolecules</a>.
</p><p>The discovery of the radioactivity of uranium ushered in additional scientific and practical uses of the element. The long <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of the isotope uranium-238 (4.51<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">9</span></sup> years) makes it well-suited for use in estimating the age of the earliest <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous rocks</a> and for other types of <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiometric dating</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Uranium%E2%80%93thorium_dating" title="Uranium–thorium dating">uranium–thorium dating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium%E2%80%93lead_dating" title="Uranium–lead dating">uranium–lead dating</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranium%E2%80%93uranium_dating" title="Uranium–uranium dating">uranium–uranium dating</a>. Uranium metal is used for <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> targets in the making of high-energy X-rays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-6"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-discovery_use">Pre-discovery use</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_as_seen_by_NASA%27s_Voyager_2.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1720" data-file-width="1720" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Uranus_as_seen_by_NASA%27s_Voyager_2.tif/lossy-page1-200px-Uranus_as_seen_by_NASA%27s_Voyager_2.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Uranus_as_seen_by_NASA%27s_Voyager_2.tif/lossy-page1-300px-Uranus_as_seen_by_NASA%27s_Voyager_2.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Uranus_as_seen_by_NASA%27s_Voyager_2.tif/lossy-page1-400px-Uranus_as_seen_by_NASA%27s_Voyager_2.tif.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranus_as_seen_by_NASA%27s_Voyager_2.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The planet <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, which uranium is named after</div></div></div>
<p>The use of uranium in its natural <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxide</a> form dates back to at least the year 79 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>, when it was used to add a yellow color to <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramic</a> glazes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-7"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup> Yellow glass with 1% uranium oxide was found in a <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> villa on Cape <a href="/wiki/Posillipo" title="Posillipo">Posillipo</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Naples" title="Gulf of Naples">Bay of Naples</a>, Italy, by R. T. Gunther of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> in 1912.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks482_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks482-25">[25]</a></sup> Starting in the late <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, pitchblende was extracted from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habsburg" title="Habsburg">Habsburg</a> silver mines in <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A1chymov" title="Jáchymov">Joachimsthal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemia</a> (now Jáchymov in the Czech Republic), and was used as a coloring agent in the local <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glassmaking</a> industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">[26]</a></sup> In the early 19th century, the world's only known sources of uranium ore were these mines.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Becquerel_plate.jpg"><img alt="Two fuzzy black features on a fuzzy white paper-like background. There is a handwriting at the top of the picture." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="397" data-file-width="492" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Becquerel_plate.jpg/220px-Becquerel_plate.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Becquerel_plate.jpg/330px-Becquerel_plate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Becquerel_plate.jpg/440px-Becquerel_plate.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Becquerel_plate.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Henri_Becquerel" title="Henri Becquerel">Antoine Henri Becquerel</a> discovered the phenomenon of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactivity</a> by exposing a <a href="/wiki/Photographic_plate" title="Photographic plate">photographic plate</a> to uranium in 1896.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discovery_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Discovery of the chemical elements">discovery</a> of the element is credited to the German chemist <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heinrich_Klaproth" title="Martin Heinrich Klaproth">Martin Heinrich Klaproth</a>. While he was working in his experimental laboratory in <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> in 1789, Klaproth was able to precipitate a yellow compound (likely <a href="/wiki/Sodium_diuranate" title="Sodium diuranate">sodium diuranate</a>) by dissolving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitchblende" title="Pitchblende">pitchblende</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> and neutralizing the solution with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">[26]</a></sup> Klaproth assumed the yellow substance was the oxide of a yet-undiscovered element and heated it with <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> to obtain a black powder, which he thought was the newly discovered metal itself (in fact, that powder was an oxide of uranium).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> He named the newly discovered element after the planet <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> (named after the primordial <a href="/wiki/Uranus_(mythology)" title="Uranus (mythology)">Greek god of the sky</a>), which had been discovered eight years earlier by <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1841, <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne-Melchior_P%C3%A9ligot" title="Eugène-Melchior Péligot">Eugène-Melchior Péligot</a>, Professor of Analytical Chemistry at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservatoire_National_des_Arts_et_M%C3%A9tiers" title="Conservatoire National des Arts et Métiers">Conservatoire National des Arts et Métiers</a> (Central School of Arts and Manufactures) in <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, isolated the first sample of uranium metal by heating <a href="/wiki/Uranium_tetrachloride" title="Uranium tetrachloride">uranium tetrachloride</a> with <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Henri_Becquerel" title="Henri Becquerel">Henri Becquerel</a> discovered <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactivity</a> by using uranium in 1896.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbiaEncy_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-ColumbiaEncy-14">[14]</a></sup> Becquerel made the discovery in Paris by leaving a sample of a uranium salt, K<sub>2</sub>UO<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> (potassium uranyl sulfate), on top of an unexposed <a href="/wiki/Photographic_plate" title="Photographic plate">photographic plate</a> in a drawer and noting that the plate had become "fogged".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks478_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks478-30">[30]</a></sup> He determined that a form of invisible light or rays emitted by uranium had exposed the plate.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fission_research">Fission research</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UraniumCubesLarge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="637" data-file-width="873" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/UraniumCubesLarge.jpg/220px-UraniumCubesLarge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/UraniumCubesLarge.jpg/330px-UraniumCubesLarge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/UraniumCubesLarge.jpg/440px-UraniumCubesLarge.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UraniumCubesLarge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cubes and cuboids of uranium produced during the Manhattan project</div></div></div>
<p>A team led by <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">Enrico Fermi</a> in 1934 observed that bombarding uranium with neutrons produces the emission of <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta rays</a> (<a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> or <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positrons</a> from the elements produced; see <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta particle</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem773_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem773-31">[31]</a></sup> The fission products were at first mistaken for new elements with atomic numbers 93 and 94, which the Dean of the Faculty of Rome, Orso Mario Corbino, christened <i><a href="/wiki/Ausonium" title="Ausonium">ausonium</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hesperium" title="Hesperium">hesperium</a></i>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The experiments leading to the discovery of uranium's ability to fission (break apart) into lighter elements and release <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> were conducted by <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Otto Hahn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Strassmann" title="Fritz Strassmann">Fritz Strassmann</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem773_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem773-31">[31]</a></sup> in Hahn's laboratory in Berlin. <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Lise Meitner</a> and her nephew, the physicist <a href="/wiki/Otto_Robert_Frisch" title="Otto Robert Frisch">Otto Robert Frisch</a>, published the physical explanation in February 1939 and named the process "<a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Soon after, Fermi hypothesized that the fission of uranium might release enough neutrons to sustain a fission reaction. Confirmation of this hypothesis came in 1939, and later work found that on average about 2.5 neutrons are released by each fission of the rare uranium isotope uranium-235.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem773_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem773-31">[31]</a></sup> Fermi urged <a href="/wiki/Alfred_O._C._Nier" title="Alfred O. C. Nier">Alfred O. C. Nier</a> to separate uranium isotopes for determination of the fissile component, and on February 29, 1940, Nier used an instrument he built at the University of Minnesota to separate the world's first <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a> sample in the Tate Laboratory. After mailed to <a href="/wiki/Pupin_Hall" title="Pupin Hall">Columbia University</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Cyclotron" title="Cyclotron">cyclotron</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_R._Dunning" title="John R. Dunning">John Dunning</a> confirmed the sample to be the isolated fissile material on March 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Further work found that the far more common uranium-238 isotope can be <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">transmuted</a> into plutonium, which, like uranium-235, is also fissile by thermal neutrons. These discoveries led numerous countries to begin working on the development of nuclear weapons and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a>.
</p><p>On 2 December 1942, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>, another team led by Enrico Fermi was able to initiate the first artificial self-sustained <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Pile-1" title="Chicago Pile-1">Chicago Pile-1</a>. An initial plan using enriched uranium-235 was abandoned as it was as yet unavailable in sufficient quantities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Working in a lab below the stands of <a href="/wiki/Stagg_Field" title="Stagg Field">Stagg Field</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a>, the team created the conditions needed for such a reaction by piling together 400 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Short_tons" title="Short tons">short tons</a> (360 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_tons" title="Metric tons">metric tons</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a>, 58 short tons (53 metric tons) of <a href="/wiki/Uranium_oxide" title="Uranium oxide">uranium oxide</a>, and six short tons (5.5 metric tons) of uranium metal, a majority of which was supplied by <a href="/wiki/Westinghouse_Lamp_Plant" title="Westinghouse Lamp Plant">Westinghouse Lamp Plant</a> in a makeshift production process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem773_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem773-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_weaponry">Nuclear weaponry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atomic_cloud_over_Hiroshima_-_NARA_542192_-_Edit.jpg"><img alt="White fragmentred mushroom-like smoke cloud evolving from the ground." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2943" data-file-width="2498" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Atomic_cloud_over_Hiroshima_-_NARA_542192_-_Edit.jpg/220px-Atomic_cloud_over_Hiroshima_-_NARA_542192_-_Edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Atomic_cloud_over_Hiroshima_-_NARA_542192_-_Edit.jpg/330px-Atomic_cloud_over_Hiroshima_-_NARA_542192_-_Edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Atomic_cloud_over_Hiroshima_-_NARA_542192_-_Edit.jpg/440px-Atomic_cloud_over_Hiroshima_-_NARA_542192_-_Edit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atomic_cloud_over_Hiroshima_-_NARA_542192_-_Edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mushroom_cloud" title="Mushroom cloud">mushroom cloud</a> over <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a> after the dropping of the uranium-based atomic bomb nicknamed '<a href="/wiki/Little_Boy" title="Little Boy">Little Boy</a>'</div></div></div>
<p>Two major types of atomic bombs were developed by the United States during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>: a uranium-based device (codenamed "<a href="/wiki/Little_Boy" title="Little Boy">Little Boy</a>") whose fissile material was highly <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">enriched uranium</a>, and a plutonium-based device (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinity_test" title="Trinity test">Trinity test</a> and "<a href="/wiki/Fat_Man" title="Fat Man">Fat Man</a>") whose plutonium was derived from uranium-238. The uranium-based Little Boy device became the first nuclear weapon used in war when it was detonated over the <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japanese</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a> on 6 August 1945. Exploding with a yield equivalent to 12,500 tonnes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinitrotoluene" title="Trinitrotoluene">TNT</a>, the blast and thermal wave of the bomb destroyed nearly 50,000 buildings and killed approximately 75,000 people (see <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks478_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks478-30">[30]</a></sup> Initially it was believed that uranium was relatively rare, and that <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">nuclear proliferation</a> could be avoided by simply buying up all known uranium stocks, but within a decade large deposits of it were discovered in many places around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactors">Reactors</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg"><img alt="An industrial room with four large illuminated light bulbs hanging down from a bar." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="373" data-file-width="500" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/220px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/330px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg/440px-First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_four_nuclear_lit_bulbs.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Four light bulbs lit with electricity generated from the first artificial electricity-producing nuclear reactor, <a href="/wiki/Experimental_Breeder_Reactor_I" title="Experimental Breeder Reactor I">EBR-I</a> (1951)</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/X-10_Graphite_Reactor" title="X-10 Graphite Reactor">X-10 Graphite Reactor</a> at <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">Oak Ridge National Laboratory</a> (ORNL) in Oak Ridge, Tennessee, formerly known as the Clinton Pile and X-10 Pile, was the world's second artificial nuclear reactor (after Enrico Fermi's Chicago Pile) and was the first reactor designed and built for continuous operation. <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">Argonne National Laboratory</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Experimental_Breeder_Reactor_I" title="Experimental Breeder Reactor I">Experimental Breeder Reactor I</a>, located at the Atomic Energy Commission's National Reactor Testing Station near <a href="/wiki/Arco,_Idaho" title="Arco, Idaho">Arco, Idaho</a>, became the first nuclear reactor to create electricity on 20 December 1951.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Initially, four 150-watt light bulbs were lit by the reactor, but improvements eventually enabled it to power the whole facility (later, the town of Arco became the first in the world to have all its <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> come from nuclear power generated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BORAX-III" title="BORAX-III">BORAX-III</a>, another reactor designed and operated by <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">Argonne National Laboratory</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The world's first commercial scale nuclear power station, <a href="/wiki/Obninsk_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Obninsk Nuclear Power Plant">Obninsk</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, began generation with its reactor AM-1 on 27 June 1954. Other early nuclear power plants were <a href="/wiki/Sellafield" title="Sellafield">Calder Hall</a> in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, which began generation on 17 October 1956,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-44">[44]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Shippingport_Atomic_Power_Station" title="Shippingport Atomic Power Station">Shippingport Atomic Power Station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, which began on 26 May 1958. Nuclear power was used for the first time for propulsion by a <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarine</a>, the <a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">USS <i>Nautilus</i></a>, in 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem773_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem773-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric_naturally_occurring_fission">Prehistoric naturally occurring fission</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Natural_nuclear_fission_reactor" title="Natural nuclear fission reactor">Natural nuclear fission reactor</a></div>
<p>In 1972, the French physicist <a href="/wiki/Francis_Perrin" title="Francis Perrin">Francis Perrin</a> discovered fifteen ancient and no longer active natural nuclear fission reactors in three separate ore deposits at the <a href="/wiki/Oklo" title="Oklo">Oklo</a> mine in <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, collectively known as the <a href="/wiki/Natural_nuclear_fission_reactor" title="Natural nuclear fission reactor">Oklo Fossil Reactors</a>. The ore deposit is 1.7 billion years old; then, uranium-235 constituted about 3% of the total uranium on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCRWM_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-OCRWM-46">[46]</a></sup> This is high enough to permit a sustained nuclear fission chain reaction to occur, provided other supporting conditions exist. The capacity of the surrounding sediment to contain the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" title="Nuclear waste">nuclear waste</a> products has been cited by the U.S. federal government as supporting evidence for the feasibility to store spent nuclear fuel at the <a href="/wiki/Yucca_Mountain_nuclear_waste_repository" title="Yucca Mountain nuclear waste repository">Yucca Mountain nuclear waste repository</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OCRWM_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-OCRWM-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contamination_and_the_Cold_War_legacy">Contamination and the Cold War legacy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg"><img alt="A graph showing evolution of number of nuclear weapons in the US and USSR and in the period 1945–2005. US dominates early and USSR later years with and crossover around 1978." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="555" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/220px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/330px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg/440px-US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_and_USSR_nuclear_stockpiles.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. and USSR/Russian nuclear weapons stockpiles, 1945–2005</div></div></div>
<p>Above-ground <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_testing" title="Nuclear testing">nuclear tests</a> by the Soviet Union and the United States in the 1950s and early 1960s and by <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> into the 1970s and 1980s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks480_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks480-19">[19]</a></sup> spread a significant amount of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fallout" title="Nuclear fallout">fallout</a> from uranium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daughter_isotope" title="Daughter isotope">daughter isotopes</a> around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Additional fallout and pollution occurred from several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_and_radiation_accidents" title="Nuclear and radiation accidents">nuclear accidents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Uranium miners have a higher incidence of <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>. An excess risk of lung cancer among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Navajo_people" title="Navajo people">Navajo</a> uranium miners, for example, has been documented and linked to their occupation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Radiation_Exposure_Compensation_Act" title="Radiation Exposure Compensation Act">Radiation Exposure Compensation Act</a>, a 1990 law in the US, required $100,000 in "compassion payments" to uranium miners diagnosed with cancer or other respiratory ailments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ajph.org_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-ajph.org-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> between the Soviet Union and the United States, huge stockpiles of uranium were amassed and tens of thousands of nuclear weapons were created using enriched uranium and plutonium made from uranium. Since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collapse_of_the_Soviet_Union_(1985%E2%80%931991)#Dissolution_of_the_USSR" title="Collapse of the Soviet Union (1985–1991)">break-up of the Soviet Union</a> in 1991, an estimated 600 short tons (540 metric tons) of highly enriched weapons grade uranium (enough to make 40,000 nuclear warheads) have been stored in often inadequately guarded facilities in the <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russian Federation</a> and several other former Soviet states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyIntel_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-EncyIntel-15">[15]</a></sup> Police in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> on at least 16 occasions from 1993 to 2005 have <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_espionage" title="Nuclear espionage">intercepted shipments</a> of smuggled bomb-grade uranium or plutonium, most of which was from ex-Soviet sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyIntel_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-EncyIntel-15">[15]</a></sup> From 1993 to 2005 the Material Protection, Control, and Accounting Program, operated by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">federal government of the United States</a>, spent approximately <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US $</a>550 million to help safeguard uranium and plutonium stockpiles in Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyIntel_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-EncyIntel-15">[15]</a></sup> This money was used for improvements and security enhancements at research and storage facilities. <i>Scientific American</i> reported in February 2006 that in some of the facilities security consisted of chain link fences which were in severe states of disrepair. According to an interview from the article, one facility had been storing samples of enriched (weapons grade) uranium in a broom closet before the improvement project; another had been keeping track of its stock of nuclear warheads using index cards kept in a shoe box.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thwarting_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-thwarting-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span></h3>
<p>Along with all elements having <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">atomic weights</a> higher than that of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, uranium is only naturally formed in <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Primordial thorium and uranium are only produced in the <a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">r-process</a> (rapid neutron capture), because the <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a> (slow neutron capture) is too slow and cannot pass the gap of instability after bismuth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B2FH_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-B2FH-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Besides the two extant primordial uranium isotopes, <sup>235</sup>U and <sup>238</sup>U, the r-process also produced significant quantities of <a href="/wiki/Uranium-236" title="Uranium-236"><sup>236</sup>U</a>, which has a shorter half-life and has long since decayed completely to <sup>232</sup>Th, which was itself enriched by the decay of <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-244" title="Plutonium-244"><sup>244</sup>Pu</a>, accounting for the observed higher-than-expected abundance of thorium and lower-than-expected abundance of uranium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thoruranium_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-thoruranium-55">[55]</a></sup> While the natural abundance of uranium has been supplemented by the decay of extinct <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-242" title="Plutonium-242"><sup>242</sup>Pu</a> (half-life 0.375 million years) and <sup>247</sup>Cm (half-life 16 million years), producing <sup>238</sup>U and <sup>235</sup>U respectively, this occurred to an almost negligible extent due to the shorter half-lives of these parents and their lower production than <sup>236</sup>U and <sup>244</sup>Pu, the parents of thorium: the <sup>247</sup>Cm:<sup>235</sup>U ratio at the formation of the Solar System was <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6995700000000000000♠</span>(7.0<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>1.6)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−5</sup></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biotic_and_abiotic">Biotic and abiotic</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uranium_in_the_environment" title="Uranium in the environment">Uranium in the environment</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pichblende.jpg"><img alt="A shiny gray 5-centimeter piece of matter with a rough surface." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="279" data-file-width="320" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Pichblende.jpg/220px-Pichblende.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0b/Pichblende.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pichblende.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uraninite, also known as pitchblende, is the most common ore mined to extract uranium.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="653" data-file-width="899" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg/220px-Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg/330px-Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg/440px-Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_Earth%27s_radiogenic_heat.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The evolution of Earth's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiogenic_heat" title="Radiogenic heat">radiogenic heat</a> flow over time: contribution from <sup>235</sup>U in red and from <sup>238</sup>U in green</div></div></div>
<p>Uranium is a <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">naturally occurring</a> element that can be found in low levels within all rock, soil, and water. Uranium is the 51st element in order of <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_elements_in_Earth%27s_crust" title="Abundance of elements in Earth's crust">abundance</a> in the Earth's crust. Uranium is also the highest-numbered element to be found naturally in significant quantities on Earth and is almost always found combined with other elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-8"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup> The decay of uranium, <a href="/wiki/Thorium" title="Thorium">thorium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40">potassium-40</a> in the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> is thought to be the main source of heat<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> that keeps the <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_Earth" title="Structure of the Earth">outer core</a> liquid and drives <a href="/wiki/Mantle_convection" title="Mantle convection">mantle convection</a>, which in turn drives <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>.
</p><p>Uranium's average concentration in the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crust</a> is (depending on the reference) 2 to 4 parts per million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciTechEncy_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-SciTechEncy-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks480_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks480-19">[19]</a></sup> or about 40 times as abundant as <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbiaEncy_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-ColumbiaEncy-14">[14]</a></sup> The Earth's crust from the surface to 25 km (15 mi) down is calculated to contain 10<sup>17</sup> kg (2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">17</span></sup> lb) of uranium while the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a> may contain 10<sup>13</sup> kg (2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">13</span></sup> lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciTechEncy_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-SciTechEncy-8">[8]</a></sup> The concentration of uranium in soil ranges from 0.7 to 11 parts per million (up to 15 parts per million in farmland soil due to use of phosphate <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>), and its concentration in sea water is 3 parts per billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks480_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks480-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Uranium is more plentiful than <a href="/wiki/Antimony" title="Antimony">antimony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, or silver, and it is about as abundant as <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-9"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks480_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks480-19">[19]</a></sup> Uranium is found in hundreds of minerals, including uraninite (the most common uranium <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a>), <a href="/wiki/Carnotite" title="Carnotite">carnotite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Autunite" title="Autunite">autunite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranophane" title="Uranophane">uranophane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Torbernite" title="Torbernite">torbernite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coffinite" title="Coffinite">coffinite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-10"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup> Significant concentrations of uranium occur in some substances such as <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> rock deposits, and minerals such as <a href="/wiki/Lignite" title="Lignite">lignite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Monazite" title="Monazite">monazite</a> sands in uranium-rich ores<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-11"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup> (it is recovered commercially from sources with as little as 0.1% uranium<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbiaEncy_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-ColumbiaEncy-14">[14]</a></sup>).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Citrobacter_freundii.jpg"><img alt="Five rod-shaped cells on a flat surface: four in a group and one separate." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="462" data-file-width="700" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Citrobacter_freundii.jpg/220px-Citrobacter_freundii.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Citrobacter_freundii.jpg/330px-Citrobacter_freundii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Citrobacter_freundii.jpg/440px-Citrobacter_freundii.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Citrobacter_freundii.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Citrobacter" title="Citrobacter">Citrobacter</a></i> species can have concentrations of uranium in their cells 300 times the level of the surrounding environment.</div></div></div><p> Some bacteria, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Shewanella_putrefaciens" title="Shewanella putrefaciens">Shewanella putrefaciens</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Geobacter_metallireducens" title="Geobacter metallireducens">Geobacter metallireducens</a></i> and some strains of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burkholderia_fungorum" title="Burkholderia fungorum">Burkholderia fungorum</a></i>, use uranium for their growth and convert U(VI) to U(IV).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other organisms, such as the lichen <i>Trapelia involuta</i> or <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bacterium" title="Bacterium">bacterium</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Citrobacter" title="Citrobacter">Citrobacter</a></i>, can absorb concentrations of uranium that are up to 300 times the level of their environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> <i>Citrobacter</i> species absorb <a href="/wiki/Uranyl" title="Uranyl">uranyl</a> ions when given <a href="/wiki/Glycerol_phosphate" title="Glycerol phosphate">glycerol phosphate</a> (or other similar organic phosphates). After one day, one gram of bacteria can encrust themselves with nine grams of uranyl phosphate crystals; this creates the possibility that these organisms could be used in <a href="/wiki/Bioremediation" title="Bioremediation">bioremediation</a> to <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_contamination" title="Radioactive contamination">decontaminate</a> uranium-polluted water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
The proteobacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Geobacter" title="Geobacter">Geobacter</a></i> has also been shown to bioremediate uranium in ground water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersonVrionis2003_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-AndersonVrionis2003-63">[63]</a></sup> The mycorrhizal fungus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glomus_intraradices" title="Glomus intraradices">Glomus intraradices</a> increases uranium content in the roots of its symbiotic plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>In nature, uranium(VI) forms highly soluble carbonate complexes at alkaline pH. This leads to an increase in mobility and availability of uranium to groundwater and soil from nuclear wastes which leads to health hazards. However, it is difficult to precipitate uranium as phosphate in the presence of excess carbonate at alkaline pH. A <i>Sphingomonas</i> sp. strain BSAR-1 has been found to express a high activity alkaline phosphatase (PhoK) that has been applied for bioprecipitation of uranium as uranyl phosphate species from alkaline solutions. The precipitation ability was enhanced by overexpressing PhoK protein in <i>E. coli</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">Plants</a> absorb some uranium from soil. Dry weight concentrations of uranium in plants range from 5 to 60 parts per billion, and ash from burnt wood can have concentrations up to 4 parts per million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">[26]</a></sup> Dry weight concentrations of uranium in <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a> plants are typically lower with one to two micrograms per day ingested through the food people eat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-6"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_and_mining">Production and mining</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">Uranium mining</a></div>
