<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:U_production-demand.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="940" data-file-width="1400" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/U_production-demand.png/220px-U_production-demand.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/U_production-demand.png/330px-U_production-demand.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/U_production-demand.png/440px-U_production-demand.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:U_production-demand.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World uranium production (mines) and demand<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA-WUM_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-WNA-WUM-66">[66]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yellowcake.jpg"><img alt="A yellow sand-like rhombic mass on black background." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="613" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Yellowcake.jpg/220px-Yellowcake.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Yellowcake.jpg/330px-Yellowcake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Yellowcake.jpg/440px-Yellowcake.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yellowcake.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yellowcake" title="Yellowcake">Yellowcake</a> is a concentrated mixture of uranium oxides that is further refined to extract pure uranium.</div></div></div>
<p>Worldwide production of U<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub> (yellowcake) in 2013 amounted to 70,015 <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>, of which 22,451 t (32%) was mined in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>. Other important uranium mining countries are <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> (9,331 t), <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> (6,350 t), <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> (4,518 t), <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a> (4,323 t) and <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> (3,135 t).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WNA-WUM_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-WNA-WUM-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>Uranium ore is mined in several ways: by <a href="/wiki/Open-pit_mining" title="Open-pit mining">open pit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Underground_mining_(soft_rock)" title="Underground mining (soft rock)">underground</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-situ_leach" title="In-situ leach">in-situ leaching</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Borehole_mining" title="Borehole mining">borehole mining</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Uranium_mining" title="Uranium mining">uranium mining</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks479_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks479-6">[6]</a></sup> Low-grade uranium ore mined typically contains 0.01 to 0.25% uranium oxides. Extensive measures must be employed to extract the metal from its ore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem774_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem774-67">[67]</a></sup> High-grade ores found in <a href="/wiki/Athabasca_Basin" title="Athabasca Basin">Athabasca Basin</a> deposits in <a href="/wiki/Saskatchewan" title="Saskatchewan">Saskatchewan</a>, Canada can contain up to 23% uranium oxides on average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Uranium ore is crushed and rendered into a fine powder and then leached with either an <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Leachate" title="Leachate">leachate</a> is subjected to one of several sequences of precipitation, solvent extraction, and ion exchange. The resulting mixture, called <a href="/wiki/Yellowcake" title="Yellowcake">yellowcake</a>, contains at least 75% uranium oxides U<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>. Yellowcake is then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcined" title="Calcined">calcined</a> to remove impurities from the milling process before refining and conversion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commercial-grade uranium can be produced through the <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduction</a> of uranium <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halides</a> with <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metal" title="Alkaline earth metal">alkaline earth metals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-12"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup> Uranium metal can also be prepared through <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of <span class="chemf nowrap">KUF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span> or
<a href="/wiki/Uranium_tetrafluoride" title="Uranium tetrafluoride"><span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></a>, dissolved in molten <a href="/wiki/Calcium_chloride" title="Calcium chloride">calcium chloride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Ca<a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">Cl</a><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">Na</a>Cl) solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-13"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup> Very pure uranium is produced through the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_decomposition" title="Thermal decomposition">thermal decomposition</a> of uranium halides on a hot filament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-14"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resources_and_reserves">Resources and reserves</span></h3>
<p>It is estimated that 5.5 million tonnes of uranium exists in ore reserves that are economically viable at US$59 per lb of uranium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-70">[70]</a></sup> while 35 million tonnes are classed as mineral resources (reasonable prospects for eventual economic extraction).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEAResourcesDemand_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAEAResourcesDemand-71">[71]</a></sup> Prices went from about $10/lb in May 2003 to $138/lb in July 2007. This has caused a big increase in spending on exploration,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-70">[70]</a></sup> with US$200 million being spent worldwide in 2005, a 54% increase on the previous year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEAResourcesDemand_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-IAEAResourcesDemand-71">[71]</a></sup> This trend continued through 2006, when expenditure on exploration rocketed to over $774 million, an increase of over 250% compared to 2004. The <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Energy_Agency" title="Nuclear Energy Agency">Nuclear Energy Agency</a> said exploration figures for 2007 would likely match those for 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Australia has 31% of the world's known uranium ore reserves<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and the world's largest single uranium deposit, located at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olympic_Dam,_South_Australia" title="Olympic Dam, South Australia">Olympic Dam</a> Mine in <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> There is a significant reserve of uranium
in <a href="/wiki/Bakouma" title="Bakouma">Bakouma</a> a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-prefecture" title="Sub-prefecture">sub-prefecture</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Prefecture" title="Prefecture">prefecture</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mbomou" title="Mbomou">Mbomou</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>.
</p><p>Some nuclear fuel comes from nuclear weapons being dismantled,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> such as from the <a href="/wiki/Megatons_to_Megawatts_Program" title="Megatons to Megawatts Program">Megatons to Megawatts Program</a>.
</p><p>An additional 4.6 billion tonnes of uranium are estimated to be in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_water" title="Sea water">sea water</a> (<a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japanese</a> scientists in the 1980s showed that extraction of uranium from sea water using <a href="/wiki/Ion_exchange" title="Ion exchange">ion exchangers</a> was technically feasible).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UseaWater_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-UseaWater-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordCohen_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordCohen-76">[76]</a></sup> There have been experiments to extract uranium from sea water,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> but the yield has been low due to the carbonate present in the water. In 2012, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ORNL" title="ORNL">ORNL</a> researchers announced the successful development of a new absorbent material dubbed HiCap which performs surface retention of solid or gas molecules, atoms or ions and also effectively removes toxic metals from water, according to results verified by researchers at Pacific Northwest National Laboratory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supplies">Supplies</span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Ambox current red.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Ambox_current_red.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <small><i>(September 2016)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uranium_market" title="Uranium market">Uranium market</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MonthlyUraniumSpot.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="641" data-file-width="979" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/MonthlyUraniumSpot.png/220px-MonthlyUraniumSpot.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/MonthlyUraniumSpot.png/330px-MonthlyUraniumSpot.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/MonthlyUraniumSpot.png/440px-MonthlyUraniumSpot.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MonthlyUraniumSpot.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Monthly uranium spot price in US$ per pound. The <a href="/wiki/Uranium_bubble_of_2007" title="Uranium bubble of 2007">2007 price peak</a> is clearly visible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uraniumingo_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-uraniumingo-80">[80]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 2005, seventeen countries produced concentrated uranium oxides: <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> (27.9% of world production), <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> (22.8%), <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> (10.5%), <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> (8.0%), <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a> (7.5%), <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> (7.4%), <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> (5.5%), the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (2.5%), <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> (2.1%), <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> (1.9%) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">China</a> (1.7%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Kazakhstan continues to increase production and may have become the world's largest producer of uranium by 2009 with an expected production of 12,826 tonnes, compared to Canada with 11,100 t and Australia with 9,430 t.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In the late 1960s, UN geologists also discovered major uranium deposits and other rare mineral reserves in <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>. The find was the largest of its kind, with industry experts estimating the deposits at over 25% of the world's then known uranium reserves of 800,000 tons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bufais_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bufais-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The ultimate available supply is believed to be sufficient for at least the next 85 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAEAResourcesDemand_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-IAEAResourcesDemand-71">[71]</a></sup> although some studies indicate underinvestment in the late twentieth century may produce supply problems in the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MITfuelSupply_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-MITfuelSupply-85">[85]</a></sup>
Uranium deposits seem to be log-normal distributed. There is a 300-fold increase in the amount of uranium recoverable for each tenfold decrease in ore grade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
In other words, there is little high grade ore and proportionately much more low grade ore available.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_reactions.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="821" data-file-width="1143" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Uranium_reactions.svg/350px-Uranium_reactions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Uranium_reactions.svg/525px-Uranium_reactions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Uranium_reactions.svg/700px-Uranium_reactions.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_reactions.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reactions of uranium metal</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxidation_states_and_oxides">Oxidation states and oxides</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides">Oxides</span></h4>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:333px;max-width:333px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:U3O8lattice.jpg"><img alt="Ball and stick model of layered crystal structure containing two types of atoms." data-file-height="493" data-file-width="817" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/U3O8lattice.jpg/200px-U3O8lattice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/U3O8lattice.jpg/300px-U3O8lattice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/U3O8lattice.jpg/400px-U3O8lattice.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:127px;max-width:127px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UO2lattice.jpg"><img alt="Ball and stick model of cubic-like crystal structure containing two types of atoms." data-file-height="743" data-file-width="821" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/UO2lattice.jpg/125px-UO2lattice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/UO2lattice.jpg/188px-UO2lattice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/UO2lattice.jpg/250px-UO2lattice.jpg 2x" width="125"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/Triuranium_octoxide" title="Triuranium octoxide">Triuranium octoxide</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Uranium_dioxide" title="Uranium dioxide">uranium dioxide</a> (right) are the two most common uranium oxides.</div></div></div>
<p>Calcined uranium yellowcake, as produced in many large mills, contains a distribution of uranium oxidation species in various forms ranging from most oxidized to least oxidized. Particles with short residence times in a calciner will generally be less oxidized than those with long retention times or particles recovered in the stack scrubber. Uranium content is usually referenced to <span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span></span>, which dates to the days of the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> when <span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span></span> was used as an analytical chemistry reporting standard.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">Phase relationships</a> in the uranium-oxygen system are complex. The most important oxidation states of uranium are uranium(IV) and uranium(VI), and their two corresponding <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a> are, respectively, <a href="/wiki/Uranium_dioxide" title="Uranium dioxide">uranium dioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Uranium_trioxide" title="Uranium trioxide">uranium trioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem779_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem779-87">[87]</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Uranium_oxide" title="Uranium oxide">uranium oxides</a> such as uranium monoxide (UO), diuranium pentoxide (<span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>), and uranium peroxide (<span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>·2H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>) also exist.
</p><p>The most common forms of uranium oxide are <a href="/wiki/Triuranium_octoxide" title="Triuranium octoxide">triuranium octoxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span></span>) and <span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANL-Chem_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-ANL-Chem-88">[88]</a></sup> Both oxide forms are solids that have low solubility in water and are relatively stable over a wide range of environmental conditions. Triuranium octoxide is (depending on conditions) the most stable compound of uranium and is the form most commonly found in nature. Uranium dioxide is the form in which uranium is most commonly used as a nuclear reactor fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANL-Chem_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-ANL-Chem-88">[88]</a></sup> At ambient temperatures, <span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> will gradually convert to <span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span></span>. Because of their stability, uranium oxides are generally considered the preferred chemical form for storage or disposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANL-Chem_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-ANL-Chem-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aqueous_chemistry">Aqueous chemistry</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:U_Oxstufen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="403" data-file-width="450" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/U_Oxstufen.jpg/150px-U_Oxstufen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/U_Oxstufen.jpg/225px-U_Oxstufen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/U_Oxstufen.jpg/300px-U_Oxstufen.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:U_Oxstufen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Uranium in its oxidation states III, IV, V, VI</div></div></div>
<p>Salts of many <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> of uranium are water-<a href="/wiki/Solubility" title="Solubility">soluble</a> and may be studied in <a href="/wiki/Aqueous_solution" title="Aqueous solution">aqueous solutions</a>. The most common ionic forms are <span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> (brown-red), <span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> (green), <span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> (unstable), and <span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> (yellow), for U(III), U(IV), U(V), and U(VI), respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem778_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem778-89">[89]</a></sup> A few <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a> and semi-metallic compounds such as UO and US exist for the formal oxidation state uranium(II), but no simple ions are known to exist in solution for that state. Ions of <span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> liberate <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> from <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> and are therefore considered to be highly unstable. The <span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> ion represents the uranium(VI) state and is known to form compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Uranyl_carbonate" title="Uranyl carbonate">uranyl carbonate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranyl_chloride" title="Uranyl chloride">uranyl chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranyl_sulfate" title="Uranyl sulfate">uranyl sulfate</a>. <span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> also forms <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_(chemistry)" title="Complex (chemistry)">complexes</a> with various <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic</a> <a href="/wiki/Chelation" title="Chelation">chelating</a> agents, the most commonly encountered of which is <a href="/wiki/Uranyl_acetate" title="Uranyl acetate">uranyl acetate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem778_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem778-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike the uranyl salts of uranium and <a href="/wiki/Polyatomic_ion" title="Polyatomic ion">polyatomic ion</a> uranium-oxide cationic forms, the <a href="/wiki/Uranate" title="Uranate">uranates</a>, salts containing a polyatomic uranium-oxide anion, are generally not water-soluble.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbonates">Carbonates</span></h4>
<p>The interactions of carbonate anions with uranium(VI) cause the <a href="/wiki/Pourbaix_diagram" title="Pourbaix diagram">Pourbaix diagram</a> to change greatly when the medium is changed from water to a carbonate containing solution. While the vast majority of carbonates are insoluble in water (students are often taught that all carbonates other than those of alkali metals are insoluble in water), uranium carbonates are often soluble in water. This is because a U(VI) cation is able to bind two terminal oxides and three or more carbonates to form anionic complexes.
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_pH">Effects of pH</span></h4>
<p>The uranium fraction diagrams in the presence of carbonate illustrate this further: when the pH of a uranium(VI) solution increases, the uranium is converted to a hydrated uranium oxide hydroxide and at high pHs it becomes an anionic hydroxide complex.
</p><p>When carbonate is added, uranium is converted to a series of carbonate complexes if the pH is increased. One effect of these reactions is increased solubility of uranium in the pH range 6 to 8, a fact that has a direct bearing on the long term stability of spent uranium dioxide nuclear fuels.
</p>
<h3><span id="Hydrides.2C_carbides_and_nitrides"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrides,_carbides_and_nitrides">Hydrides, carbides and nitrides</span></h3>
<p>Uranium metal heated to 250 to 300 °C (482 to 572 °F) reacts with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hydride" title="Uranium hydride">uranium hydride</a>. Even higher temperatures will reversibly remove the hydrogen. This property makes uranium hydrides convenient starting materials to create reactive uranium powder along with various uranium <a href="/wiki/Carbide" title="Carbide">carbide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitride" title="Nitride">nitride</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halide</a> compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-91">[91]</a></sup> Two crystal modifications of uranium hydride exist: an α form that is obtained at low temperatures and a β form that is created when the formation temperature is above 250 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Uranium_carbide" title="Uranium carbide">Uranium carbides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranium_nitride" title="Uranium nitride">uranium nitrides</a> are both relatively <a href="/wiki/Chemically_inert" title="Chemically inert">inert</a> <a href="/wiki/Semimetal" title="Semimetal">semimetallic</a> compounds that are minimally soluble in <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>, react with water, and can ignite in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">air</a> to form <span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">8</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-91">[91]</a></sup> Carbides of uranium include uranium monocarbide (U<a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">C</a>), uranium dicarbide (<span class="chemf nowrap">UC<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), and diuranium tricarbide (<span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>). Both UC and <span class="chemf nowrap">UC<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> are formed by adding carbon to molten uranium or by exposing the metal to <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> at high temperatures. Stable below 1800 °C, <span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> is prepared by subjecting a heated mixture of UC and <span class="chemf nowrap">UC<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> to mechanical stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem780_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem780-92">[92]</a></sup> Uranium nitrides obtained by direct exposure of the metal to <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> include uranium mononitride (UN), uranium dinitride (<span class="chemf nowrap">UN<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>), and diuranium trinitride (<span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem780_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem780-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides">Halides</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg"><img alt="Snow-like substance in a sealed glass ampoule." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="683" height="87" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg/220px-Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg/330px-Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg/440px-Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Uranium_hexafluoride_crystals_sealed_in_an_ampoule.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">Uranium hexafluoride</a> is the feedstock used to separate uranium-235 from natural uranium.</div></div></div>
<p>All uranium fluorides are created using <a href="/wiki/Uranium_tetrafluoride" title="Uranium tetrafluoride">uranium tetrafluoride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>); <span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> itself is prepared by hydrofluorination of uranium dioxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_91-3"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-91">[91]</a></sup> Reduction of <span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> with hydrogen at 1000 °C produces uranium trifluoride (<span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>). Under the right conditions of temperature and pressure, the reaction of solid <span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> with gaseous <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">uranium hexafluoride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>) can form the intermediate fluorides of <span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">9</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">U<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">17</sub></span></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">5</sub></span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_91-4"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>At room temperatures, <span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> has a high <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">vapor pressure</a>, making it useful in the <a href="/wiki/Gaseous_diffusion" title="Gaseous diffusion">gaseous diffusion</a> process to separate the rare uranium-235 from the common uranium-238 isotope. This compound can be prepared from uranium dioxide and uranium hydride by the following process:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_91-5"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 4 HF → <span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> (500 °C, endothermic)</dd>
<dd><span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span> (350 °C, endothermic)</dd></dl>
<p>The resulting <span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>, a white solid, is highly <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">reactive</a> (by fluorination), easily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry)" title="Sublimation (chemistry)">sublimes</a> (emitting a vapor that behaves as a nearly <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">ideal gas</a>), and is the most volatile compound of uranium known to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_91-6"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>One method of preparing <a href="/wiki/Uranium_tetrachloride" title="Uranium tetrachloride">uranium tetrachloride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">UCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) is to directly combine <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> with either uranium metal or uranium hydride. The reduction of <span class="chemf nowrap">UCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> by hydrogen produces uranium trichloride (<span class="chemf nowrap">UCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) while the higher chlorides of uranium are prepared by reaction with additional chlorine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_91-7"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-91">[91]</a></sup> All uranium chlorides react with water and air.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bromide" title="Bromide">Bromides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iodide" title="Iodide">iodides</a> of uranium are formed by direct reaction of, respectively, <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a> with uranium or by adding <span class="chemf nowrap">UH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> to those element's acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_91-8"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-91">[91]</a></sup> Known examples include: <span class="chemf nowrap">UBr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">UBr<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">UI<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">UI<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>. Uranium oxyhalides are water-soluble and include <span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>F<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">UOCl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, <span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Cl<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>, and <span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>Br<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. Stability of the oxyhalides decrease as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a> of the component halide increases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem782_91-9"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem782-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_concentrations">Natural concentrations</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_uranium" title="Isotopes of uranium">Isotopes of uranium</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Natural_uranium" title="Natural uranium">Natural uranium</a> consists of three major <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>: <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">uranium-238</a> (99.28% <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">natural abundance</a>), uranium-235 (0.71%), and <a href="/wiki/Uranium-234" title="Uranium-234">uranium-234</a> (0.0054%). All three are <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive</a>, emitting <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a>, with the exception that all three of these isotopes have small probabilities of undergoing <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_fission" title="Spontaneous fission">spontaneous fission</a>, rather than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_emission" title="Alpha emission">alpha emission</a>. There are also five other trace isotopes: uranium-239, which is formed when <sup>238</sup>U undergoes spontaneous fission, releasing neutrons that are captured by another <sup>238</sup>U atom; uranium-237, which is formed when <sup>238</sup>U captures a neutron but emits two more, which then decays to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neptunium-237" title="Neptunium-237">neptunium-237</a>; and finally, uranium-233, which is formed in the <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">decay chain</a> of that neptunium-237. It is also expected that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thorium-232" title="Thorium-232">thorium-232</a> should be able to undergo <a href="/wiki/Double_beta_decay" title="Double beta decay">double beta decay</a>, which would produce uranium-232, but this has not yet been observed experimentally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NUBASE_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-NUBASE-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Uranium-238 is the most stable isotope of uranium, with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of about 4.468<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">9</span></sup> years, roughly the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">age of the Earth</a>. Uranium-235 has a half-life of about 7.13<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">8</span></sup> years, and uranium-234 has a half-life of about 2.48<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">5</span></sup> years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem777_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem777-94">[94]</a></sup>
For natural uranium, about 49% of its alpha rays are emitted by each of <sup>238</sup>U atom, and also 49% by <sup>234</sup>U (since the latter is formed from the former) and about 2.0% of them by the <sup>235</sup>U. When the Earth was young, probably about one-fifth of its uranium was uranium-235, but the percentage of <sup>234</sup>U was probably much lower than this.
</p><p>Uranium-238 is usually an α emitter (occasionally, it undergoes spontaneous fission), decaying through the <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain#Uranium_series" title="Decay chain">uranium series</a>, which has 18 members, into <a href="/wiki/Lead#Isotopes" title="Lead">lead-206</a>, by a variety of different decay paths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbiaEncy_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-ColumbiaEncy-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain" title="Decay chain">decay chain</a> of <sup>235</sup>U, which is called the <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain#Actinium_series" title="Decay chain">actinium series</a>, has 15 members and eventually decays into lead-207.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbiaEncy_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-ColumbiaEncy-14">[14]</a></sup> The constant rates of decay in these decay series makes the comparison of the ratios of parent to <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">daughter elements</a> useful in <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiometric dating</a>.
</p><p>Uranium-234, which is a member of the uranium series (the decay chain of uranium-238), decays to lead-206 through a series of relatively short-lived isotopes.
</p><p>Uranium-233 is made from <a href="/wiki/Thorium#Isotopes" title="Thorium">thorium-232</a> by neutron bombardment, usually in a nuclear reactor, and <sup>233</sup>U is also fissile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-15"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup> Its decay chain forms part of the <a href="/wiki/Decay_chain#Neptunium_series" title="Decay chain">neptunium series</a> and ends at <a href="/wiki/Bismuth-209" title="Bismuth-209">bismuth-209</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thallium" title="Thallium">thallium</a>-205.
</p><p>Uranium-235 is important for both <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>, because it is the only uranium isotope existing in nature on Earth in any significant amount that is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fissile" title="Fissile">fissile</a>. This means that it can be split into two or three fragments (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fission_products" title="Fission products">fission products</a>) by thermal neutrons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColumbiaEncy_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-ColumbiaEncy-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Uranium-238 is not fissile, but is a fertile isotope, because after <a href="/wiki/Neutron_activation" title="Neutron activation">neutron activation</a> it can produce <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-239" title="Plutonium-239">plutonium-239</a>, another fissile isotope. Indeed, the <sup>238</sup>U nucleus can absorb one neutron to produce the radioactive isotope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranium-239" title="Uranium-239">uranium-239</a>. <sup>239</sup>U decays by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta_emission" title="Beta emission">beta emission</a> to <a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">neptunium</a>-239, also a beta-emitter, that decays in its turn, within a few days into plutonium-239. <sup>239</sup>Pu was used as fissile material in the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bomb</a> detonated in the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trinity_test" title="Trinity test">Trinity test</a>" on 15 July 1945 in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncyChem773_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-EncyChem773-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Enrichment">Enrichment</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Enriched_uranium" title="Enriched uranium">Enriched uranium</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg"><img alt="A photo of a large hall filled with arrays of long white standing cylinders." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="3200" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg/220px-Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg/330px-Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg/440px-Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gas_centrifuge_cascade.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cascades of <a href="/wiki/Gas_centrifuge" title="Gas centrifuge">gas centrifuges</a> are used to enrich uranium ore to concentrate its fissionable isotopes.</div></div></div>
<p>In nature, uranium is found as uranium-238 (99.2742%) and uranium-235 (0.7204%). <a href="/wiki/Isotope_separation" title="Isotope separation">Isotope separation</a> concentrates (enriches) the fissionable uranium-235 for nuclear weapons and most nuclear power plants, except for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gas_cooled_reactor" title="Gas cooled reactor">gas cooled reactors</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressurised_heavy_water_reactor" title="Pressurised heavy water reactor">pressurised heavy water reactors</a>. Most neutrons released by a fissioning atom of uranium-235 must impact other uranium-235 atoms to sustain the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a>. The concentration and amount of uranium-235 needed to achieve this is called a '<a href="/wiki/Critical_mass" title="Critical mass">critical mass</a>'.
</p><p>To be considered 'enriched', the uranium-235 fraction should be between 3% and 5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> This process produces huge quantities of uranium that is depleted of uranium-235 and with a correspondingly increased fraction of uranium-238, called depleted uranium or 'DU'. To be considered 'depleted', the uranium-235 isotope concentration should be no more than 0.3%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paducah_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-paducah-96">[96]</a></sup> The price of uranium has risen since 2001, so enrichment tailings containing more than 0.35% uranium-235 are being considered for re-enrichment, driving the price of depleted uranium hexafluoride above $130 per kilogram in July 2007 from $5 in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paducah_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-paducah-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gas_centrifuge" title="Gas centrifuge">gas centrifuge</a> process, where gaseous <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">uranium hexafluoride</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">UF<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span></span>) is separated by the difference in molecular weight between <sup>235</sup>UF<sub>6</sub> and <sup>238</sup>UF<sub>6</sub> using high-speed <a href="/wiki/Centrifuge" title="Centrifuge">centrifuges</a>, is the cheapest and leading enrichment process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks478_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks478-30">[30]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gaseous_diffusion" title="Gaseous diffusion">gaseous diffusion</a> process had been the leading method for enrichment and was used in the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a>. In this process, uranium hexafluoride is repeatedly <a href="/wiki/Diffusion" title="Diffusion">diffused</a> through a <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>-<a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> membrane, and the different isotopes of uranium are separated by diffusion rate (since uranium 238 is heavier it diffuses slightly slower than uranium-235).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks478_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks478-30">[30]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Molecular_laser_isotope_separation" title="Molecular laser isotope separation">molecular laser isotope separation</a> method employs a <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a> beam of precise energy to sever the bond between uranium-235 and fluorine. This leaves uranium-238 bonded to fluorine and allows uranium-235 metal to precipitate from the solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks479_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks479-6">[6]</a></sup> An alternative laser method of enrichment is known as <a href="/wiki/Atomic_vapor_laser_isotope_separation" title="Atomic vapor laser isotope separation">atomic vapor laser isotope separation</a> (AVLIS) and employs visible <a href="/wiki/Tunable_laser" title="Tunable laser">tunable lasers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Dye_laser" title="Dye laser">dye lasers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Another method used is liquid thermal diffusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SciTechEncy_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-SciTechEncy-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_exposure">Human exposure</span></h2>
<p>A person can be exposed to uranium (or its <a href="/wiki/Decay_product" title="Decay product">radioactive daughters</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a>) by inhaling dust in air or by ingesting contaminated water and food. The amount of uranium in air is usually very small; however, people who work in factories that process <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a> <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, live near government facilities that made or tested nuclear weapons, live or work near a modern battlefield where depleted uranium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weapons" title="Weapons">weapons</a> have been used, or live or work near a <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>-fired power plant, facilities that mine or process uranium ore, or enrich uranium for reactor fuel, may have increased exposure to uranium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA-Rad_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-Rad-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATSDR-ToxFAQ_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-ATSDR-ToxFAQ-99">[99]</a></sup> Houses or structures that are over uranium deposits (either natural or man-made slag deposits) may have an increased incidence of exposure to radon gas. The <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> (OSHA) has set the <a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">permissible exposure limit</a> for uranium exposure in the workplace as 0.25 mg/m<sup>3</sup> over an 8-hour workday. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) has set a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) of 0.2 mg/m<sup>3</sup> over an 8-hour workday and a short-term limit of 0.6 mg/m<sup>3</sup>. At levels of 10 mg/m<sup>3</sup>, uranium is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IDLH" title="IDLH">immediately dangerous to life and health</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most ingested uranium is excreted during <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestion</a>. Only 0.5% is absorbed when insoluble forms of uranium, such as its oxide, are ingested, whereas absorption of the more soluble <a href="/wiki/Uranyl" title="Uranyl">uranyl</a> ion can be up to 5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-7"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">[26]</a></sup> However, soluble uranium compounds tend to quickly pass through the body, whereas insoluble uranium compounds, especially when inhaled by way of dust into the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>, pose a more serious exposure hazard. After entering the bloodstream, the absorbed uranium tends to <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioaccumulate</a> and stay for many years in <a href="/wiki/Bone" title="Bone">bone</a> tissue because of uranium's affinity for phosphates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-8"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">[26]</a></sup> Uranium is not absorbed through the skin, and <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a> released by uranium cannot penetrate the skin.
</p><p>Incorporated uranium becomes <a href="/wiki/Uranyl" title="Uranyl">uranyl</a> ions, which accumulate in bone, liver, kidney, and reproductive tissues. Uranium can be decontaminated from steel surfaces<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Francis_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Francis-101">[101]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wu_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wu-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_and_precautions">Effects and precautions</span></h3>
<p>Normal functioning of the <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>, and other systems can be affected by uranium exposure, because, besides being weakly radioactive, uranium is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic_metal" title="Toxic metal">toxic metal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks477_26-9"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks477-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Craft04_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Craft04-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATSDR_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-ATSDR-104">[104]</a></sup> Uranium is also a reproductive toxicant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hindin2005_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hindin2005-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Radiological effects are generally local because alpha radiation, the primary form of <sup>238</sup>U decay, has a very short range, and will not penetrate skin. Alpha radiation from inhaled uranium has been demonstrated to cause lung cancer in exposed nuclear workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Uranyl" title="Uranyl">Uranyl</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">UO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) ions, such as from <a href="/wiki/Uranium_trioxide" title="Uranium trioxide">uranium trioxide</a> or uranyl nitrate and other hexavalent uranium compounds, have been shown to cause birth defects and immune system damage in laboratory animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> While the CDC has published one study that no human <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> has been seen as a result of exposure to natural or depleted uranium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATSDR-PHS_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-ATSDR-PHS-109">[109]</a></sup> exposure to uranium and its decay products, especially <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a>, are widely known and significant health threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BuildingBlocks480_19-5"><a href="#cite_note-BuildingBlocks480-19">[19]</a></sup> Exposure to <a href="/wiki/Strontium-90" title="Strontium-90">strontium-90</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iodine-131" title="Iodine-131">iodine-131</a>, and other fission products is unrelated to uranium exposure, but may result from medical procedures or exposure to spent reactor fuel or fallout from nuclear weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
Although accidental inhalation exposure to a high concentration of <a href="/wiki/Uranium_hexafluoride" title="Uranium hexafluoride">uranium hexafluoride</a> has
resulted in human fatalities, those deaths were associated with the generation of highly toxic hydrofluoric acid and <a href="/wiki/Uranyl_fluoride" title="Uranyl fluoride">uranyl fluoride</a> rather than with uranium itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Finely divided uranium metal presents a fire hazard because uranium is <a href="/wiki/Pyrophoricity" title="Pyrophoricity">pyrophoric</a>; small grains will ignite spontaneously in air at room temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LANL_9-16"><a href="#cite_note-LANL-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Uranium metal is commonly handled with gloves as a sufficient precaution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOH.WA_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-DOH.WA-112">[112]</a></sup> Uranium concentrate is handled and contained so as to ensure that people do not inhale or ingest it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOH.WA_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-DOH.WA-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
